The number of ways to choose 3 digits from 10 is given by the combination:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{10}{3} = \\frac{10 \\times 9 \\times 8}{3 \\times 2 \\times 1} = 120\n\\]\n\nOnce we have chosen 3 distinct digits, there is exactly one way to arrange them in descending order. Therefore, the number of 3-digit descending numbers is simply the number of ways to choose the 3 digits, which is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{120}\n\\]'
"To determine how many distinct values are possible for the expression \\(8 + 4 \\times 6 \\div 2\\) with one set of parentheses, we need to consider all possible placements of the parentheses. The expression can be modified in the following ways:\n\n1. \\((8 + 4) \\times 6 \\div 2\\)\n2. \\(8 + (4 \\times 6) \\div 2\\)\n3. \\(8 + 4 \\times (6 \\div 2)\\)\n4. \\((8 + 4 \\times 6) \\div 2\\)\n5. \\(8 + (4 \\times 6 \\div 2)\\)\n\nNow, let's evaluate each expression:\n\n1. \\((8 + 4) \\times 6 \\div 2 = 12 \\times 6 \\div 2 = 72 \\div 2 = 36\\)\n\n2. \\(8 + (4 \\times 6) \\div 2 = 8 + 24 \\div 2 = 8 + 12 = 20\\)\n\n3. \\(8 + 4 \\times (6 \\div 2) = 8 + 4 \\times 3 = 8 + 12 = 20\\)\n\n4. \\((8 + 4 \\times 6) \\div 2 = (8 + 24) \\div 2 = 32 \\div 2 = 16\\)\n\n5. \\(8 + (4 \\times 6 \\div 2) = 8 + (24 \\div 2) = 8 + 12 = 20\\)\n\nThe distinct values obtained from these evaluations are 36, 20, and 16. Therefore, there are \\(\\boxed{3}\\) distinct values possible for the expression with one set of parentheses."
'To form an international commission, we need to select one country from each of the four continents: Europe, Asia, North America, and Africa. \n\n- For Europe, we have 6 options.\n- For Asia, we have 4 options.\n- For North America, we have 3 options.\n- For Africa, we have 7 options.\n\nThe total number of ways to form the commission is the product of the number of choices for each continent. Therefore, the total number of ways is:\n\n\\[ 6 \\times 4 \\times 3 \\times 7 = 504. \\]\n\nThus, there are \\(\\boxed{504}\\) ways to form the international commission.'
"To solve this problem, we first calculate the total number of 3-letter words that can be formed using the 26 letters of the Roman alphabet. Each position in the word can be filled by any of the 26 letters, so the total number of 3-letter words is:\n\n\\[ 26 \\times 26 \\times 26 = 26^3 = 17,576. \\]\n\nNext, we calculate the number of 3-letter words that do not contain the letter 'A' at all. For each position in the word, we can choose from the remaining 25 letters (since 'A' is not allowed), so the number of such words is:\n\n\\[ 25 \\times 25 \\times 25 = 25^3 = 15,625. \\]\n\nFinally, to find the number of 3-letter words that contain the letter 'A' at least once, we subtract the number of words without 'A' from the total number of words:\n\n\\[ 17,576 - 15,625 = 1,951. \\]\n\nTherefore, the number of 3-letter words that contain the letter 'A' at least once is \\(\\boxed{1,951}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to find the smallest value of \\( k \\) such that the probability of drawing one white ball and one black ball is \\(\\frac{10}{21}\\).\n\nFirst, calculate the total number of ways to draw 2 balls from the bin. There are \\(5\\) white balls and \\(k\\) black balls, so the total number of balls is \\(5 + k\\). The number of ways to choose 2 balls from \\(5 + k\\) balls is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{5+k}{2} = \\frac{(5+k)(4+k)}{2}\n\\]\n\nNext, calculate the number of ways to draw one white ball and one black ball. There are 5 ways to choose 1 white ball from 5 white balls, and \\(k\\) ways to choose 1 black ball from \\(k\\) black balls. Therefore, the number of ways to choose one white and one black ball is:\n\n\\[\n5 \\times k\n\\]\n\nThe probability of drawing one white ball and one black ball is then:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{5k}{\\binom{5+k}{2}} = \\frac{5k}{\\frac{(5+k)(4+k)}{2}} = \\frac{10k}{(5+k)(4+k)}\n\\]\n\nWe are given that this probability is \\(\\frac{10}{21}\\). Therefore, we set up the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{10k}{(5+k)(4+k)} = \\frac{10}{21}\n\\]\n\nCross-multiply to solve for \\(k\\):\n\n\\[\n10k \\cdot 21 = 10 \\cdot (5+k)(4+k)\n\\]\n\nSimplify:\n\n\\[\n210k = 10(20 + 9k + k^2)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n210k = 200 + 90k + 10k^2\n\\]\n\nRearrange the terms:\n\n\\[\n10k^2 - 120k + 200 = 0\n\\]\n\nDivide the entire equation by 10:\n\n\\[\nk^2 - 12k + 20 = 0\n\\]\n\nNow, solve the quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nk = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = -12\\), and \\(c = 20\\). Calculate the discriminant:\n\n\\[\nb^2 - 4ac = (-12)^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot 20 = 144 - 80 = 64\n\\]\n\nThe square root of 64 is 8. Substitute into the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nk = \\frac{12 \\pm 8}{2}\n\\]\n\nThis gives two solutions:\n\n\\[\nk = \\frac{12 + 8}{2} = 10 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad k = \\frac{12 - 8}{2} = 2\n\\]\n\nThe smallest possible value of \\(k\\) is \\(\\boxed{2}\\).'
'To find the probability that a boy and a girl are chosen, we can use the concept of combinations to determine the number of favorable outcomes and the total number of possible outcomes.\n\nFirst, calculate the total number of ways to choose 2 members from the 20 members in the club:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{20}{2} = \\frac{20 \\times 19}{2 \\times 1} = 190\n\\]\n\nNext, calculate the number of ways to choose 1 boy and 1 girl. There are 12 boys and 8 girls, so the number of ways to choose 1 boy and 1 girl is:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{12}{1} \\times \\binom{8}{1} = 12 \\times 8 = 96\n\\]\n\nThe probability that a boy and a girl are chosen is the number of favorable outcomes divided by the total number of possible outcomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{96}{190} = \\frac{48}{95}\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability that a boy and a girl are chosen is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{48}{95}}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to consider the number of ways to choose two socks of the same color from the given socks.\n\n1. **White Socks**: There are 8 white socks. The number of ways to choose 2 white socks from 8 is given by the combination formula \\(\\binom{n}{k}\\), where \\(n\\) is the total number of items to choose from, and \\(k\\) is the number of items to choose. So, for white socks, we have:\n   \\[\n   \\binom{8}{2} = \\frac{8 \\times 7}{2 \\times 1} = 28\n   \\]\n\n2. **Brown Socks**: There are 6 brown socks. The number of ways to choose 2 brown socks from 6 is:\n   \\[\n   \\binom{6}{2} = \\frac{6 \\times 5}{2 \\times 1} = 15\n   \\]\n\n3. **Blue Socks**: There are 4 blue socks. The number of ways to choose 2 blue socks from 4 is:\n   \\[\n   \\binom{4}{2} = \\frac{4 \\times 3}{2 \\times 1} = 6\n   \\]\n\nFinally, we add up all the combinations to find the total number of ways to choose a pair of socks of the same color:\n\\[\n28 + 15 + 6 = 49\n\\]\n\nTherefore, there are \\(\\boxed{49}\\) ways to choose a pair of socks of the same color.'
'The word "TEPEE" consists of 5 letters where the letter E is repeated 3 times. To find the number of distinct arrangements of the letters, we use the formula for permutations of multiset:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{n!}{n_1! \\times n_2! \\times \\cdots \\times n_k!}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( n \\) is the total number of letters, and \\( n_1, n_2, \\ldots, n_k \\) are the frequencies of the repeated letters.\n\nIn the word "TEPEE":\n- The total number of letters \\( n = 5 \\).\n- The letter E is repeated 3 times.\n\nThus, the number of distinct arrangements is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{5!}{3!} = \\frac{120}{6} = 20\n\\]\n\nTherefore, there are \\(\\boxed{20}\\) distinct ways to arrange the letters of the word "TEPEE".'
"To solve this problem, we need to find the number of integers between 1 and 2005 that are multiples of 3 or 4 but not multiples of 12.\n\nFirst, let's find the number of multiples of 3 between 1 and 2005. A number is a multiple of 3 if it can be expressed as \\(3k\\) for some integer \\(k\\). The largest integer \\(k\\) such that \\(3k \\leq 2005\\) is \\(\\left\\lfloor \\frac{2005}{3} \\right\\rfloor = 668\\). Therefore, there are 668 multiples of 3.\n\nNext, let's find the number of multiples of 4 between 1 and 2005. A number is a multiple of 4 if it can be expressed as \\(4m\\) for some integer \\(m\\). The largest integer \\(m\\) such that \\(4m \\leq 2005\\) is \\(\\left\\lfloor \\frac{2005}{4} \\right\\rfloor = 501\\). Therefore, there are 501 multiples of 4.\n\nNow, let's find the number of multiples of 12 between 1 and 2005. A number is a multiple of 12 if it can be expressed as \\(12n\\) for some integer \\(n\\). The largest integer \\(n\\) such that \\(12n \\leq 2005\\) is \\(\\left\\lfloor \\frac{2005}{12} \\right\\rfloor = 167\\). Therefore, there are 167 multiples of 12.\n\nTo find the number of integers that are multiples of 3 or 4, we use the principle of inclusion-exclusion:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Multiples of 3 or 4} = (\\text{Multiples of 3}) + (\\text{Multiples of 4}) - (\\text{Multiples of 12})\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the values we found:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Multiples of 3 or 4} = 668 + 501 - 167 = 1002\n\\]\n\nTherefore, there are 1002 numbers between 1 and 2005 that are multiples of 3 or 4.\n\nFinally, since we want the numbers that are multiples of 3 or 4 but not 12, we subtract the multiples of 12 from this total:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Multiples of 3 or 4 but not 12} = 1002 - 167 = 835\n\\]\n\nThus, there are \\(\\boxed{835}\\) numbers between 1 and 2005 that are multiples of 3 or 4 but not 12."
'The word "NINE" consists of 4 letters, where the letter \'N\' appears twice. To find the number of distinct arrangements of the letters, we use the formula for permutations of multiset:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{n!}{n_1! \\cdot n_2! \\cdot \\ldots \\cdot n_k!}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( n \\) is the total number of letters, and \\( n_1, n_2, \\ldots, n_k \\) are the frequencies of the distinct letters.\n\nIn the word "NINE":\n- The total number of letters \\( n = 4 \\).\n- The letter \'N\' appears 2 times.\n- The letter \'I\' appears 1 time.\n- The letter \'E\' appears 1 time.\n\nThus, the number of distinct arrangements is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4!}{2! \\cdot 1! \\cdot 1!} = \\frac{24}{2} = 12\n\\]\n\nTherefore, there are \\(\\boxed{12}\\) distinct ways to arrange the letters of the word "NINE".'
"To solve this problem, we need to find the probability that the game is won before the fourth turn. The game is won when the die shows a 1 or a 6 and the coin shows heads.\n\nFirst, let's calculate the probability of winning on a single turn. The probability of rolling a 1 or a 6 on a standard die is \\(\\frac{2}{6} = \\frac{1}{3}\\). The probability of getting heads on a fair coin is \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\). Therefore, the probability of winning on a single turn is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{3} \\times \\frac{1}{2} = \\frac{1}{6}\n\\]\n\nNext, we need to find the probability of winning before the fourth turn. This means winning on the first, second, or third turn. We can use the complement rule to make this calculation easier. First, we find the probability of not winning on a single turn, which is:\n\n\\[\n1 - \\frac{1}{6} = \\frac{5}{6}\n\\]\n\nThe probability of not winning in all three turns (i.e., losing the first three turns) is:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{5}{6}\\right)^3 = \\frac{125}{216}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the probability of winning before the fourth turn is the complement of losing all three turns:\n\n\\[\n1 - \\frac{125}{216} = \\frac{216}{216} - \\frac{125}{216} = \\frac{91}{216}\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability that the game will be won before the fourth turn is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{91}{216}}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to find the probability that Gimli flips more heads than Legolas when Legolas flips a fair coin 2008 times and Gimli flips a fair coin 2009 times.\n\nLet's denote the number of heads Legolas gets as \\( L \\) and the number of heads Gimli gets as \\( G \\). We are interested in finding the probability that \\( G > L \\).\n\nSince both Legolas and Gimli are flipping fair coins, the number of heads each gets follows a binomial distribution. Specifically, \\( L \\sim \\text{Binomial}(2008, 0.5) \\) and \\( G \\sim \\text{Binomial}(2009, 0.5) \\).\n\nA key insight here is to use symmetry and the fact that the distributions are approximately normal due to the large number of flips. The expected number of heads for Legolas is \\( \\frac{2008}{2} = 1004 \\) and for Gimli is \\( \\frac{2009}{2} = 1004.5 \\).\n\nThe variance for Legolas is \\( \\frac{2008}{4} = 502 \\) and for Gimli is \\( \\frac{2009}{4} = 502.25 \\).\n\nThe problem is symmetric around the point where both have the same expected number of heads. However, since Gimli flips one more coin than Legolas, he has a slight advantage. This symmetry and the fact that the distributions are approximately normal suggest that the probability that Gimli gets more heads than Legolas is slightly greater than 0.5.\n\nTo be more precise, we can use the normal approximation to the binomial distribution. The difference \\( D = G - L \\) can be approximated by a normal distribution with mean \\( \\mu_D = 0.5 \\) (since \\( G \\) has one more expected head than \\( L \\)) and variance \\( \\sigma_D^2 = 502.25 + 502 = 1004.25 \\).\n\nThe probability that \\( G > L \\) is equivalent to the probability that \\( D > 0 \\). Using the normal approximation:\n\n\\[\nP(D > 0) = P\\left( \\frac{D - 0.5}{\\sqrt{1004.25}} > \\frac{0 - 0.5}{\\sqrt{1004.25}} \\right)\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\nP\\left( Z > -\\frac{0.5}{\\sqrt{1004.25}} \\right)\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( Z \\) is a standard normal variable. The value \\( -\\frac{0.5}{\\sqrt{1004.25}} \\) is very close to 0, so the probability is slightly greater than 0.5.\n\nTherefore, the probability that Gimli flips more heads than Legolas is approximately \\(\\boxed{0.5}\\)."
"To form a three-digit odd number using the digits 3, 4, 5, and 6 without repetition, we need to ensure that the last digit is odd. The available odd digits are 3 and 5. Let's consider each case separately:\n\n**Case 1: The last digit is 3.**\n\n- The hundreds place can be filled by any of the remaining digits: 4, 5, or 6. So, there are 3 choices for the hundreds place.\n- The tens place can be filled by any of the remaining 2 digits after choosing the hundreds place. So, there are 2 choices for the tens place.\n\nThus, for this case, the number of different numbers is:\n\\[ 3 \\times 2 = 6. \\]\n\n**Case 2: The last digit is 5.**\n\n- The hundreds place can be filled by any of the remaining digits: 3, 4, or 6. So, there are 3 choices for the hundreds place.\n- The tens place can be filled by any of the remaining 2 digits after choosing the hundreds place. So, there are 2 choices for the tens place.\n\nThus, for this case, the number of different numbers is:\n\\[ 3 \\times 2 = 6. \\]\n\nAdding the results from both cases, the total number of different three-digit odd numbers that can be formed is:\n\\[ 6 + 6 = 12. \\]\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{12}\\)."
'To find the probability that Stu pulls out the letters M, A, T, H in that specific order, we need to consider the sequence of events and the total number of possible outcomes.\n\n1. **Probability of drawing M first:**\n   - There are 7 cards, and only 1 of them is M. So, the probability of drawing M first is \\(\\frac{1}{7}\\).\n\n2. **Probability of drawing A second:**\n   - After drawing M, there are 6 cards left, and only 1 of them is A. So, the probability of drawing A second is \\(\\frac{1}{6}\\).\n\n3. **Probability of drawing T third:**\n   - After drawing M and A, there are 5 cards left, and only 1 of them is T. So, the probability of drawing T third is \\(\\frac{1}{5}\\).\n\n4. **Probability of drawing H fourth:**\n   - After drawing M, A, and T, there are 4 cards left, and only 1 of them is H. So, the probability of drawing H fourth is \\(\\frac{1}{4}\\).\n\nTo find the overall probability of drawing M, A, T, H in that order, we multiply the probabilities of each step:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{7} \\times \\frac{1}{6} \\times \\frac{1}{5} \\times \\frac{1}{4} = \\frac{1}{840}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the probability that Stu pulls out the letters M, A, T, H in that order is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{840}}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to consider the constraints and the structure of the triforce. The triforce consists of four equilateral triangles: one large triangle composed of three smaller triangles at the corners and one smaller triangle in the center.\n\nLet's label the triangles as follows:\n- A: the top triangle\n- B: the bottom-left triangle\n- C: the bottom-right triangle\n- D: the center triangle\n\nThe constraints are that no two triangles sharing a side can be the same color. This means:\n- A cannot be the same color as B, C, or D.\n- B cannot be the same color as A, C, or D.\n- C cannot be the same color as A, B, or D.\n- D cannot be the same color as A, B, or C.\n\nGiven that we have three colors (gold, black, and green), we can start by coloring triangle A. There are 3 choices for the color of A.\n\nOnce A is colored, we move to B. B shares a side with A, so it cannot be the same color as A. Therefore, there are 2 choices for the color of B.\n\nNext, we color C. C shares a side with both A and B, so it cannot be the same color as either. Since A and B are already colored with two different colors, C must be colored with the remaining color. Thus, there is 1 choice for the color of C.\n\nFinally, we color D. D shares sides with A, B, and C. Since A, B, and C are all different colors, D must be colored with a color different from all three. However, since we only have three colors, D must be the same color as one of the other triangles. This is a contradiction, indicating that our initial assumption of using only three colors is incorrect.\n\nTherefore, we need to use a different approach. Let's consider the possibility of using a different coloring strategy:\n\n1. Color A with any of the 3 colors.\n2. Color B with any of the 2 remaining colors.\n3. Color C with the remaining color.\n4. Color D with any of the 2 colors that are not the same as A.\n\nThis gives us:\n- 3 choices for A\n- 2 choices for B\n- 1 choice for C\n- 2 choices for D\n\nThe total number of ways to color the triforce is:\n\\[ 3 \\times 2 \\times 1 \\times 2 = 12. \\]\n\nThus, there are \\(\\boxed{12}\\) different ways to color the triforce."
'To solve this problem, we need to calculate the probability that after the second set of rolls, the five dice show a "full house" configuration. Let\'s break down the process:\n\n1. **Initial Roll:**\n   - We roll five dice. Two of them show the same value, and the other three show different values.\n   - Let\'s denote the value of the pair as \\( A \\) and the values of the other three dice as \\( B \\), \\( C \\), and \\( D \\).\n\n2. **Re-roll the Three Different Dice:**\n   - We re-roll the dice showing \\( B \\), \\( C \\), and \\( D \\).\n\n3. **Probability of a Full House:**\n   - A full house requires three dice to show the same value and the other two to show the same value.\n   - After the re-roll, we want either:\n     - Three dice to show the value \\( A \\) and two dice to show another value (say \\( E \\)).\n     - Three dice to show a new value (say \\( F \\)) and two dice to show the value \\( A \\).\n\n4. **Calculating the Probability:**\n   - **Case 1:** Three dice show the value \\( A \\).\n     - Probability that one of the re-rolled dice shows \\( A \\) is \\(\\frac{1}{6}\\).\n     - Probability that two of the re-rolled dice show \\( A \\) is \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{6}\\right)^2 = \\frac{1}{36}\\).\n     - Probability that all three re-rolled dice show \\( A \\) is \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{6}\\right)^3 = \\frac{1}{216}\\).\n     - We need exactly one of the re-rolled dice to show \\( A \\) (since we already have two showing \\( A \\)), so we use the binomial coefficient to find the number of ways to choose which one shows \\( A \\): \\(\\binom{3}{1} = 3\\).\n     - Probability for this case: \\(3 \\times \\frac{1}{6} \\times \\left(\\frac{5}{6}\\right)^2 = 3 \\times \\frac{1}{6} \\times \\frac{25}{36} = \\frac{75}{216}\\).\n\n   - **Case 2:** Three dice show a new value \\( F \\).\n     - Probability that one of the re-rolled dice shows a new value \\( F \\) is \\(\\frac{5}{6}\\).\n     - Probability that two of the re-rolled dice show \\( F \\) is \\(\\left(\\frac{5}{6}\\right)^2 = \\frac{25}{36}\\).\n     - Probability that all three re-rolled dice show \\( F \\) is \\(\\left(\\frac{5}{6}\\right)^3 = \\frac{125}{216}\\).\n     - We need exactly two of the re-rolled dice to show the same new value \\( F \\), so we use the binomial coefficient to find the number of ways to choose which two show \\( F \\): \\(\\binom{3}{2} = 3\\).\n     - Probability for this case: \\(3 \\times \\frac{5}{6} \\times \\frac{1}{6} \\times \\frac{5}{6} = 3 \\times \\frac{5}{6} \\times \\frac{5}{36} = \\frac{75}{216}\\).\n\n5. **Total Probability:**\n   - Add the probabilities from both cases: \\(\\frac{75}{216} + \\frac{75}{216} = \\frac{150}{216} = \\frac{25}{36}\\).\n\nTherefore, the probability that after the second set of rolls, the dice show a full house is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{25}{36}}\\).'
'To find the greatest possible total number of intersection points of ten circles, we need to consider how many intersection points can occur between each pair of circles. \n\nFor any two circles, there can be at most 2 intersection points. Therefore, if we have \\( n \\) circles, the number of ways to choose 2 circles from \\( n \\) is given by the combination formula \\(\\binom{n}{2}\\). \n\nIn this case, we have 10 circles, so the number of pairs of circles is:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{10}{2} = \\frac{10 \\times 9}{2} = 45\n\\]\n\nSince each pair of circles can intersect at most 2 times, the maximum number of intersection points is:\n\n\\[\n2 \\times 45 = 90\n\\]\n\nThus, the greatest possible total number of intersection points of these ten circles is \\(\\boxed{90}\\).'

This can be calculated using combinations:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{4}{3} = 4\n\\]\n\nThis means there are 4 ways to select any three cards from the four cards.\n\nNext, we need to find the number of ways to select three cards such that the product of the numbers on those cards is zero. 'To compute \\(\\binom{11}{4}\\), we use the formula for combinations:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{n}{r} = \\frac{n!}{r!(n-r)!}\n\\]\n\nIn this case, \\(n = 11\\) and \\(r = 4\\). Plugging these values into the formula, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{11}{4} = \\frac{11!}{4!(11-4)!} = \\frac{11!}{4! \\cdot 7!}\n\\]\n\nWe can simplify this by canceling out the \\(7!\\) in the numerator and denominator:\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{11 \\times 10 \\times 9 \\times 8 \\times 7!}{4! \\times 7!} = \\frac{11 \\times 10 \\times 9 \\times 8}{4!}\n\\]\n\nNow, calculate \\(4!\\):\n\n\\[\n4! = 4 \\times 3 \\times 2 \\times 1 = 24\n\\]\n\nSubstitute back into the expression:\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{11 \\times 10 \\times 9 \\times 8}{24}\n\\]\n\nCalculate the numerator:\n\n\\[\n11 \\times 10 = 110\n\\]\n\\[\n110 \\times 9 = 990\n\\]\n\\[\n990 \\times 8 = 7920\n\\]\n\nNow divide by 24:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{7920}{24} = 330\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\binom{11}{4} = \\boxed{330}\\).'
'To find the probability that the first card is a heart (\\(\\heartsuit\\)) and the second card is a club (\\(\\clubsuit\\)), we can follow these steps:\n\n1. **Probability of the first card being a heart (\\(\\heartsuit\\)):**  \n   There are 13 hearts in a standard deck of 52 cards. Therefore, the probability that the first card is a heart is:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{13}{52} = \\frac{1}{4}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Probability of the second card being a club (\\(\\clubsuit\\)) given the first card is a heart:**  \n   After the first card is drawn, there are 51 cards left in the deck. Since the first card was a heart, all 13 clubs are still in the deck. 'To form a 7-person steering committee with 3 men and 4 women, we need to choose 3 men from the 30 available men and 4 women from the 40 available women. We can calculate the number of ways to do this using combinations.\n\nFirst, calculate the number of ways to choose 3 men from 30 men:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{30}{3} = \\frac{30 \\times 29 \\times 28}{3 \\times 2 \\times 1} = 4060\n\\]\n\nNext, calculate the number of ways to choose 4 women from 40 women:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{40}{4} = \\frac{40 \\times 39 \\times 38 \\times 37}{4 \\times 3 \\times 2 \\times 1} = 91,390\n\\]\n\nFinally, multiply these two results to find the total number of ways to form the committee:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{30}{3} \\times \\binom{40}{4} = 4060 \\times 91,390 = 371,993,400\n\\]\n\nTherefore, there are \\(\\boxed{371,993,400}\\) ways to form the committee.'
'To find the number of points with integer coordinates strictly inside the rectangular region defined by the vertices \\((5,4)\\), \\((-5,4)\\), \\((-5,-4)\\), and \\((5,-4)\\), we first determine the dimensions of the rectangle.\n\nThe length of the rectangle along the x-axis is from \\(x = -5\\) to \\(x = 5\\), which is \\(5 - (-5) = 10\\).\n\nThe length of the rectangle along the y-axis is from \\(y = -4\\) to \\(y = 4\\), which is \\(4 - (-4) = 8\\).\n\nThe rectangle includes all integer points \\((x, y)\\) where \\(-5 < x < 5\\) and \\(-4 < y < 4\\).\n\nFor the x-coordinates, the integer values strictly between \\(-5\\) and \\(5\\) are \\(-4, -3, -2, -1, 0, 1, 2, 3, 4\\). This gives us 9 possible x-values.\n\nFor the y-coordinates, the integer values strictly between \\(-4\\) and \\(4\\) are \\(-3, -2, -1, 0, 1, 2, 3\\). This gives us 7 possible y-values.\n\nThe total number of integer coordinate points strictly inside the rectangle is the product of the number of possible x-values and y-values:\n\n\\[\n9 \\times 7 = 63\n\\]\n\nThus, the number of points with integer coordinates strictly in the interior of the rectangular region is \\(\\boxed{63}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to calculate the probability that Team A is selected on at least two of the next three days. \n\nFirst, let's determine the probability that Team A is selected on any given day. Since two out of the three teams are selected each day, the probability that Team A is selected on a single day is:\n\n\\[\nP(\\text{Team A is selected}) = \\frac{2}{3}\n\\]\n\nNext, we need to find the probability that Team A is selected on at least two of the next three days. This can happen in the following scenarios:\n1. Team A is selected on exactly two days.\n2. Team A is selected on all three days.\n\nLet's calculate the probability for each scenario:\n\n1. **Team A is selected on exactly two days:**\n\n   The number of ways to choose 2 days out of 3 is given by the combination \\(\\binom{3}{2} = 3\\). The probability of Team A being selected on exactly two days is:\n\n   \\[\n   \\left(\\frac{2}{3}\\right)^2 \\times \\left(\\frac{1}{3}\\right) = \\frac{4}{9} \\times \\frac{1}{3} = \\frac{4}{27}\n   \\]\n\n   Since there are 3 different ways to choose which 2 days Team A is selected, the total probability for this scenario is:\n\n   \\[\n   3 \\times \\frac{4}{27} = \\frac{12}{27} = \\frac{4}{9}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Team A is selected on all three days:**\n\n   The probability of Team A being selected on all three days is:\n\n   \\[\n   \\left(\\frac{2}{3}\\right)^3 = \\frac{8}{27}\n   \\]\n\nFinally, we add the probabilities of these two scenarios to find the total probability that Team A is selected on at least two of the next three days:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{9} + \\frac{8}{27}\n\\]\n\nTo add these fractions, we need a common denominator. The least common multiple of 9 and 27 is 27. Converting \\(\\frac{4}{9}\\) to a fraction with a denominator of 27 gives us \\(\\frac{12}{27}\\). Therefore, the total probability is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{12}{27} + \\frac{8}{27} = \\frac{20}{27}\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability that Team A is selected on at least two of the next three days is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{20}{27}}\\)."
'Alyssa has four marbles, and she can choose to include or not include each marble in a set. For each marble, there are two choices: either include it in the set or do not include it. Therefore, the total number of ways to choose any subset of the four marbles is \\(2^4 = 16\\).\n\nHowever, this count includes the empty set, which is the case where no marbles are chosen. Since Alyssa must choose at least one marble, we need to subtract the empty set from the total count. Thus, the number of ways Alyssa can form a set with at least one marble is:\n\n\\[ 16 - 1 = 15. \\]\n\nTherefore, there are \\(\\boxed{15}\\) different ways for Alyssa to form a set consisting of some of the marbles, choosing at least one marble.'
'The expression \\(\\dbinom{4}{0}+\\dbinom{4}{1}+\\dbinom{4}{2}+\\dbinom{4}{3}+\\dbinom{4}{4}\\) represents the sum of the binomial coefficients for \\(n = 4\\). According to the binomial theorem, the sum of the binomial coefficients for a given \\(n\\) is equal to \\(2^n\\).\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\dbinom{4}{0}+\\dbinom{4}{1}+\\dbinom{4}{2}+\\dbinom{4}{3}+\\dbinom{4}{4} = 2^4\n\\]\n\nCalculating \\(2^4\\), we get:\n\n\\[\n2^4 = 16\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{16}\\).'
'To find the number of diagonals in a hexagon, we can use the formula for the number of diagonals in a polygon with \\( n \\) sides, which is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{n(n-3)}{2}\n\\]\n\nFor a hexagon, \\( n = 6 \\). Plugging this into the formula, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{6(6-3)}{2} = \\frac{6 \\times 3}{2} = \\frac{18}{2} = 9\n\\]\n\nTherefore, hexagon \\( ABCDEF \\) has \\(\\boxed{9}\\) diagonals.'
"To solve this problem, we need to determine how many items are in circle \\( B \\) but not in circle \\( C \\).\n\nLet's break down the information given:\n\n1. There are 7 items in circle \\( C \\).\n2. There are 20 items in circle \\( A \\), and 10 of those items are not in circle \\( B \\). This means that the remaining 10 items in circle \\( A \\) are in both \\( A \\) and \\( B \\).\n\nWe need to find the number of items in circle \\( B \\) but not in circle \\( C \\).\n\nLet's denote:\n- \\( x \\) as the number of items in \\( B \\) but not in \\( C \\).\n- \\( y \\) as the number of items in both \\( B \\) and \\( C \\).\n\nFrom the information given, we know:\n- The total number of items in \\( B \\) is \\( x + y \\).\n- The number of items in both \\( A \\) and \\( B \\) is 10, which includes items that might also be in \\( C \\).\n\nSince there are 7 items in \\( C \\), and \\( y \\) represents the number of items in both \\( B \\) and \\( C \\), we have:\n\\[ y \\leq 7 \\]\n\nNow, let's consider the items in \\( A \\):\n- 10 items are in \\( A \\) but not in \\( B \\).\n- 10 items are in both \\( A \\) and \\( B \\).\n\nSince the total number of items in \\( A \\) is 20, and 10 of those are not in \\( B \\), the remaining 10 must be in both \\( A \\) and \\( B \\).\n\nNow, we need to find \\( x \\), the number of items in \\( B \\) but not in \\( C \\).\n\nSince the total number of items in \\( B \\) is \\( x + y \\), and we know that 10 items are in both \\( A \\) and \\( B \\), we can assume that some of these 10 items might also be in \\( C \\).\n\nHowever, without additional information about the distribution of items between \\( A \\), \\( B \\), and \\( C \\), we cannot determine the exact value of \\( x \\) without making assumptions.\n\nIf we assume that none of the 10 items in both \\( A \\) and \\( B \\) are in \\( C \\), then \\( x = 10 \\).\n\nTherefore, the number of items in \\( B \\) but not in \\( C \\) is \\(\\boxed{10}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the probability that the product of the numbers rolled on two dice is either odd or a multiple of three.\n\nFirst, let's consider when the product is odd. For the product of two numbers to be odd, both numbers must be odd. Each die has three odd numbers (1, 3, and 5), so the probability that one die shows an odd number is \\(\\frac{3}{6} = \\frac{1}{2}\\). Therefore, the probability that both dice show odd numbers is:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{1}{2}\\right) \\times \\left(\\frac{1}{2}\\right) = \\frac{1}{4}\n\\]\n\nNext, let's consider when the product is a multiple of three. A product is a multiple of three if at least one of the numbers is a multiple of three. Each die has two numbers that are multiples of three (3 and 6), so the probability that one die shows a multiple of three is \\(\\frac{2}{6} = \\frac{1}{3}\\). The probability that neither die shows a multiple of three is:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{2}{3}\\right) \\times \\left(\\frac{2}{3}\\right) = \\frac{4}{9}\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability that at least one die shows a multiple of three is:\n\n\\[\n1 - \\frac{4}{9} = \\frac{5}{9}\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find the probability that the product is either odd or a multiple of three. We use the principle of inclusion-exclusion:\n\n\\[\nP(\\text{odd or multiple of 3}) = P(\\text{odd}) + P(\\text{multiple of 3}) - P(\\text{odd and multiple of 3})\n\\]\n\nWe already have \\(P(\\text{odd}) = \\frac{1}{4}\\) and \\(P(\\text{multiple of 3}) = \\frac{5}{9}\\).\n\nFor \\(P(\\text{odd and multiple of 3})\\), the only odd number that is a multiple of three is 3. So, both dice must show 3. The probability of this happening is:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{1}{6}\\right) \\times \\left(\\frac{1}{6}\\right) = \\frac{1}{36}\n\\]\n\nNow, we can calculate:\n\n\\[\nP(\\text{odd or multiple of 3}) = \\frac{1}{4} + \\frac{5}{9} - \\frac{1}{36}\n\\]\n\nTo add these fractions, we find a common denominator, which is 36:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{4} = \\frac{9}{36}, \\quad \\frac{5}{9} = \\frac{20}{36}, \\quad \\frac{1}{36} = \\frac{1}{36}\n\\]\n\nSo:\n\n\\[\nP(\\text{odd or multiple of 3}) = \\frac{9}{36} + \\frac{20}{36} - \\frac{1}{36} = \\frac{28}{36} = \\frac{7}{9}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the probability that Jean wins is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{7}{9}}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to consider the arrangement of 4 distinct beads on a bracelet with a knot, where reflections are considered the same due to the ability to flip the bracelet over.\n\nFirst, let's consider the number of ways to arrange 4 distinct beads in a linear sequence. This is simply \\(4!\\), which equals 24.\n\nHowever, since the beads are on a bracelet, we need to account for the fact that arrangements that are reflections of each other are considered the same. Normally, for a circular arrangement, we would divide by 2 to account for reflections, but the presence of the knot means that the arrangement is not fully circular. The knot acts as a fixed point, so we don't need to divide by the number of rotations (which would be 4 for a fully circular arrangement without a knot).\n\nThus, we only need to account for the reflection symmetry. Therefore, we divide the total number of linear arrangements by 2:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4!}{2} = \\frac{24}{2} = 12\n\\]\n\nSo, there are \\(\\boxed{12}\\) distinct ways to arrange 4 distinct beads on a bracelet with a knot, considering reflections as identical."
"To solve this problem, we need to find the probability of getting at least three heads in a row when flipping a fair coin four times. We can do this by considering all possible outcomes and counting the favorable ones.\n\nFirst, let's list all possible outcomes of flipping a coin four times. There are \\(2^4 = 16\\) possible outcomes. These are:\n\n1. HHHH\n2. HHHT\n3. HHTH\n4. HTHH\n5. THHH\n6. HTTT\n7. THTT\n8. TTHT\n9. TTTH\n10. HTTH\n11. THTH\n12. THHT\n13. TTHH\n14. HHTT\n15. TTTT\n16. TTTT\n\nNow, let's identify the outcomes that have at least three heads in a row:\n\n- HHHH (4 heads in a row)\n- HHHT (3 heads in a row)\n- HTHH (3 heads in a row)\n- THHH (3 heads in a row)\n\nThere are 4 favorable outcomes.\n\nTherefore, the probability of flipping at least three heads in a row is the number of favorable outcomes divided by the total number of outcomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{16} = \\frac{1}{4}\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability of flipping at least three heads in a row is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{4}}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to consider the nature of the ant's movement on the hexagon. The hexagon has 6 vertices, and at each step, the ant can move to one of the two adjacent vertices. We are interested in the probability that after 15 steps, the ant is back at the starting vertex.\n\nThis is a classic problem of random walks on a cycle graph, specifically a hexagon (6-cycle). The key insight is to use the concept of parity and symmetry in the cycle.\n\n1. **Parity Consideration**: \n   - The ant can only return to the starting vertex after an even number of steps. This is because each move changes the parity of the distance from the starting vertex. Since 15 is odd, the ant cannot be at the starting vertex after 15 steps.\n\n2. **Conclusion**:\n   - Since it is impossible for the ant to be at the starting vertex after 15 steps due to the parity argument, the probability is 0.\n\nTherefore, the probability that the ant is standing on the same vertex on which he began after 15 steps is \\(\\boxed{0}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to consider the two possible scenarios for the first draw and their respective probabilities:\n\n1. **First Scenario: A Red Ball is Drawn First**\n   - Probability of drawing a red ball first: \\(\\frac{4}{6} = \\frac{2}{3}\\).\n   - If a red ball is drawn, it is replaced with a white ball. This results in 3 red balls and 3 white balls in the jar.\n   - Probability of drawing a red ball in the second draw: \\(\\frac{3}{6} = \\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\n2. **Second Scenario: A White Ball is Drawn First**\n   - Probability of drawing a white ball first: \\(\\frac{2}{6} = \\frac{1}{3}\\).\n   - If a white ball is drawn, it is replaced with a red ball. This results in 5 red balls and 1 white ball in the jar.\n   - Probability of drawing a red ball in the second draw: \\(\\frac{5}{6}\\).\n\nNow, we calculate the total probability of drawing a red ball in the second draw by considering both scenarios:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Total Probability} = \\left(\\frac{2}{3} \\times \\frac{1}{2}\\right) + \\left(\\frac{1}{3} \\times \\frac{5}{6}\\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{2}{6} + \\frac{5}{18}\n\\]\n\nTo add these fractions, we find a common denominator, which is 18:\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{6}{18} + \\frac{5}{18} = \\frac{11}{18}\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability that the second ball drawn is red is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{11}{18}}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to choose 1 vowel and 2 consonants from the alphabet.\n\n1. **Choosing the vowel:** There are 5 vowels in the English alphabet: A, E, I, O, and U. We need to choose 1 vowel from these 5, which can be done in \\(\\binom{5}{1} = 5\\) ways.\n\n2. **Choosing the consonants:** There are 21 consonants in the English alphabet (since there are 26 letters in total and 5 of them are vowels). We need to choose 2 consonants from these 21, which can be done in \\(\\binom{21}{2}\\) ways. Calculating this gives:\n   \\[\n   \\binom{21}{2} = \\frac{21 \\times 20}{2 \\times 1} = 210\n   \\]\n\n3. **Calculating the total number of ways:** Since the choice of the vowel and the choice of the consonants are independent, we multiply the number of ways to choose the vowel by the number of ways to choose the consonants:\n   \\[\n   5 \\times 210 = 1050\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, there are \\(\\boxed{1050}\\) ways to choose 3 distinct letters of the alphabet, consisting of 1 vowel and 2 consonants.'
"To determine the number of possible license plates, we need to consider both the letter and digit components separately, and then multiply the possibilities together.\n\n**Letters:**\n\n1. The first letter must be a vowel. There are 5 vowels in the English alphabet: A, E, I, O, U. So, there are 5 choices for the first letter.\n2. The second letter can be any consonant (since no letter can be repeated). There are 21 consonants in the English alphabet (26 total letters minus 5 vowels). So, there are 21 choices for the second letter.\n3. The third letter can be any remaining letter that hasn't been used yet. Since two letters have already been used, there are 24 letters left to choose from. So, there are 24 choices for the third letter.\n\nThe total number of ways to choose the letters is:\n\\[ 5 \\times 21 \\times 24. \\]\n\n**Digits:**\n\n1. The first digit must be one of \\(\\{1, 2, 4, 6\\}\\). So, there are 4 choices for the first digit.\n2. The second digit can be any digit from 0 to 9, except the one used as the first digit. So, there are 9 choices for the second digit.\n3. The third digit can be any remaining digit that hasn't been used yet. Since two digits have already been used, there are 8 digits left to choose from. So, there are 8 choices for the third digit.\n\nThe total number of ways to choose the digits is:\n\\[ 4 \\times 9 \\times 8. \\]\n\n**Total Number of License Plates:**\n\nTo find the total number of possible license plates, multiply the number of ways to choose the letters by the number of ways to choose the digits:\n\\[\n(5 \\times 21 \\times 24) \\times (4 \\times 9 \\times 8).\n\\]\n\nCalculating each part:\n- Letters: \\(5 \\times 21 \\times 24 = 2520\\).\n- Digits: \\(4 \\times 9 \\times 8 = 288\\).\n\nThus, the total number of possible license plates is:\n\\[\n2520 \\times 288 = 725,760.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, there are \\(\\boxed{725760}\\) possible license plates."

For the first card, we have 26 options. After selecting the first card, there are 25 red cards remaining for the second selection.\n\nTherefore, the number of different ways to choose two red cards, considering the order, is given by multiplying the number of choices for each card:\n\n\\[ 26 \\times 25 = 650. \\]\n\nThus, there are \\(\\boxed{650}\\) different ways to choose two red cards from the deck.'
'To find the value of \\(\\frac{5! \\cdot 2!}{3!}\\), we first need to calculate the factorials involved:\n\n- \\(5! = 5 \\times 4 \\times 3 \\times 2 \\times 1 = 120\\)\n- \\(2! = 2 \\times 1 = 2\\)\n- \\(3! = 3 \\times 2 \\times 1 = 6\\)\n\nNow, substitute these values into the expression:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{5! \\cdot 2!}{3!} = \\frac{120 \\cdot 2}{6}\n\\]\n\nCalculate the numerator:\n\n\\[\n120 \\cdot 2 = 240\n\\]\n\nNow, divide by the denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{240}{6} = 40\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\frac{5! \\cdot 2!}{3!}\\) is \\(\\boxed{40}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to determine the probability that the chords \\(AB\\) and \\(CD\\) intersect when four points \\(A\\), \\(B\\), \\(C\\), and \\(D\\) are chosen randomly on the circumference of a circle.\n\nFor the chords \\(AB\\) and \\(CD\\) to intersect, the points must be arranged such that one of the following conditions is satisfied:\n- The points are in the order \\(A, C, B, D\\) around the circle.\n- The points are in the order \\(C, A, D, B\\) around the circle.\n\nIn general, for any four points on a circle, there are \\(\\binom{4}{2} = 6\\) ways to choose two pairs of points. However, only two of these arrangements will result in intersecting chords.\n\nThus, the probability that the chords \\(AB\\) and \\(CD\\) intersect is the number of favorable arrangements divided by the total number of arrangements:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Probability} = \\frac{2}{6} = \\frac{1}{3}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the probability that segments \\(AB\\) and \\(CD\\) intersect is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{3}}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to calculate the probability of two mutually exclusive events: either exactly 3 of the pieces of fruit are oranges, or exactly 6 of the pieces of fruit are apples. We will use the binomial probability formula to find the probability of each event and then add them together.\n\n1. **Probability of exactly 3 oranges:**\n\n   The probability of choosing an orange is \\( p = \\frac{1}{3} \\) since there are three types of fruit (orange, apple, banana) and the choice is random. The probability of not choosing an orange (choosing either an apple or a banana) is \\( 1 - p = \\frac{2}{3} \\).\n\n   The number of ways to choose exactly 3 oranges out of 8 pieces of fruit is given by the binomial coefficient \\(\\binom{8}{3}\\).\n\n   The probability of exactly 3 oranges is:\n   \\[\n   P(\\text{3 oranges}) = \\binom{8}{3} \\left(\\frac{1}{3}\\right)^3 \\left(\\frac{2}{3}\\right)^5\n   \\]\n\n   Calculating this:\n   \\[\n   \\binom{8}{3} = \\frac{8 \\times 7 \\times 6}{3 \\times 2 \\times 1} = 56\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   P(\\text{3 oranges}) = 56 \\times \\left(\\frac{1}{27}\\right) \\times \\left(\\frac{32}{243}\\right) = 56 \\times \\frac{32}{6561} = \\frac{1792}{6561}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Probability of exactly 6 apples:**\n\n   Similarly, the probability of choosing an apple is \\( p = \\frac{1}{3} \\), and the probability of not choosing an apple is \\( \\frac{2}{3} \\).\n\n   The number of ways to choose exactly 6 apples out of 8 pieces of fruit is given by the binomial coefficient \\(\\binom{8}{6}\\).\n\n   The probability of exactly 6 apples is:\n   \\[\n   P(\\text{6 apples}) = \\binom{8}{6} \\left(\\frac{1}{3}\\right)^6 \\left(\\frac{2}{3}\\right)^2\n   \\]\n\n   Calculating this:\n   \\[\n   \\binom{8}{6} = \\binom{8}{2} = \\frac{8 \\times 7}{2 \\times 1} = 28\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   P(\\text{6 apples}) = 28 \\times \\left(\\frac{1}{729}\\right) \\times \\left(\\frac{4}{9}\\right) = 28 \\times \\frac{4}{6561} = \\frac{112}{6561}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Total probability:**\n\n   Since these two events are mutually exclusive, we add their probabilities:\n   \\[\n   P(\\text{3 oranges or 6 apples}) = \\frac{1792}{6561} + \\frac{112}{6561} = \\frac{1904}{6561}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the probability that either exactly 3 of the pieces of fruit are oranges or exactly 6 of the pieces of fruit are apples is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1904}{6561}}\\).'
'To find the probability that all three cards dealt are hearts, we first determine the total number of ways to choose 3 cards from a standard deck of 52 cards. This is given by the combination:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{52}{3} = \\frac{52 \\times 51 \\times 50}{3 \\times 2 \\times 1} = 22,100\n\\]\n\nNext, we find the number of ways to choose 3 hearts from the 13 hearts available in the deck. This is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{13}{3} = \\frac{13 \\times 12 \\times 11}{3 \\times 2 \\times 1} = 286\n\\]\n\nThe probability that all three cards are hearts is the ratio of the number of favorable outcomes to the total number of outcomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\binom{13}{3}}{\\binom{52}{3}} = \\frac{286}{22,100}\n\\]\n\nTo simplify this fraction, we find the greatest common divisor (GCD) of 286 and 22,100. The GCD is 2, so we divide both the numerator and the denominator by 2:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{286 \\div 2}{22,100 \\div 2} = \\frac{143}{11,050}\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability that all three cards are hearts is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\frac{143}{11,050}}\n\\]'
"In a singles tournament with six players where each player plays every other player exactly once, the total number of games played is given by the combination \\(\\binom{6}{2}\\), which is the number of ways to choose 2 players from 6 to form a match. This is calculated as:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{6}{2} = \\frac{6 \\times 5}{2 \\times 1} = 15\n\\]\n\nThe problem states the number of games won by each player except Monica. Let's add up the wins for the other players:\n\n- Helen won 4 games.\n- Ines won 3 games.\n- Janet won 2 games.\n- Kendra won 2 games.\n- Lara won 2 games.\n\nAdding these, we have:\n\n\\[\n4 + 3 + 2 + 2 + 2 = 13\n\\]\n\nSince there are 15 games in total, and each game results in one win, the total number of wins must also be 15. Therefore, the number of games Monica won is:\n\n\\[\n15 - 13 = 2\n\\]\n\nThus, Monica won \\(\\boxed{2}\\) games."
"To solve this problem, we need to arrange 8 books on a shelf such that no two books of the same type are adjacent. We have three types of books: math, history, and physics. Let's denote them as M, H, and P.\n\nSince no two books of the same type can be adjacent, we need to ensure that each book is separated by books of other types. One way to approach this is to consider the arrangement of the books as a sequence of slots that need to be filled with different types of books.\n\n1. **Determine the Pattern:**\n   - We have 8 slots to fill with M, H, and P such that no two adjacent slots have the same type of book.\n   - A possible pattern that satisfies this condition is to alternate the types of books. However, with 8 slots and 3 types, a simple alternating pattern won't work directly.\n\n2. **Consider the Combinations:**\n   - We can start by considering the number of ways to fill the slots with different types of books.\n   - Since we have 3 types of books and 8 slots, we can use a permutation approach to ensure no two adjacent books are of the same type.\n\n3. **Calculate the Arrangements:**\n   - We can use a backtracking or recursive approach to count the number of valid arrangements.\n   - Start by placing a book of one type in the first slot, then choose a different type for the next slot, and continue this process while ensuring no two adjacent slots have the same type.\n\n4. **Recursive Formula:**\n   - Let \\( f(n) \\) be the number of ways to arrange \\( n \\) books such that no two adjacent books are of the same type.\n   - For the first book, we have 3 choices (M, H, or P).\n   - For each subsequent book, we have 2 choices (since it cannot be the same type as the previous book).\n   - Therefore, the recursive formula is \\( f(n) = 3 \\times 2^{n-1} \\).\n\n5. **Calculate \\( f(8) \\):**\n   - Using the formula, \\( f(8) = 3 \\times 2^{8-1} = 3 \\times 2^7 = 3 \\times 128 = 384 \\).\n\nThus, there are \\(\\boxed{384}\\) ways to arrange the 8 books on the shelf such that no two books of the same type are adjacent."
"To solve this problem, we need to consider the number of distinct arrangements of 5 different keys on a keychain, taking into account that two arrangements are considered the same if one can be rotated or flipped to match the other.\n\nFirst, let's consider the number of ways to arrange 5 keys in a line. This is simply \\(5!\\) (5 factorial), which is:\n\n\\[\n5! = 5 \\times 4 \\times 3 \\times 2 \\times 1 = 120\n\\]\n\nHowever, since the keys are on a keychain, we need to account for rotations and reflections. \n\n1. **Rotations**: For a circular arrangement, any of the 5 keys can be chosen as a starting point, so we divide by 5 to account for rotations. This gives us:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{5!}{5} = \\frac{120}{5} = 24\n\\]\n\n2. **Reflections**: A keychain can also be flipped, which means each arrangement can be mirrored. Therefore, we need to divide by 2 to account for reflections:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{24}{2} = 12\n\\]\n\nThus, the number of distinct ways to arrange 5 different keys on a keychain, considering rotations and reflections, is \\(\\boxed{12}\\)."
'To find the constant term in the expansion of \\(\\left(10x^3 - \\frac{1}{2x^2}\\right)^5\\), we use the binomial theorem. The binomial theorem states that:\n\n\\[\n(a + b)^n = \\sum_{k=0}^{n} \\binom{n}{k} a^{n-k} b^k\n\\]\n\nIn this case, \\(a = 10x^3\\), \\(b = -\\frac{1}{2x^2}\\), and \\(n = 5\\). We are looking for the term where the power of \\(x\\) is zero, i.e., the constant term.\n\nThe general term in the expansion is:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{5}{k} (10x^3)^{5-k} \\left(-\\frac{1}{2x^2}\\right)^k\n\\]\n\nSimplifying this, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{5}{k} (10)^{5-k} x^{3(5-k)} \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^k x^{-2k}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\binom{5}{k} (10)^{5-k} \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^k x^{15 - 3k - 2k}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\binom{5}{k} (10)^{5-k} \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^k x^{15 - 5k}\n\\]\n\nWe want the exponent of \\(x\\) to be zero:\n\n\\[\n15 - 5k = 0\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(k\\), we find:\n\n\\[\n15 = 5k \\implies k = 3\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(k = 3\\) into the expression for the term, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{5}{3} (10)^{5-3} \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^3\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\binom{5}{3} (10)^2 \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^3\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 10 \\cdot 100 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{8}\\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 1000 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{8}\\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -125\n\\]\n\nThus, the constant term in the expansion is \\(\\boxed{-125}\\).'
'To determine the number of distinct 5-card hands that can be dealt from a standard 52-card deck, we use the concept of combinations. The number of ways to choose 5 cards from a deck of 52 cards is given by the combination formula:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{n}{r} = \\frac{n!}{r!(n-r)!}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( n \\) is the total number of items to choose from, \\( r \\) is the number of items to choose, and \\( ! \\) denotes factorial, which is the product of all positive integers up to that number.\n\nIn this case, \\( n = 52 \\) and \\( r = 5 \\). Plugging these values into the formula, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{52}{5} = \\frac{52!}{5!(52-5)!} = \\frac{52!}{5! \\times 47!}\n\\]\n\nWe can simplify this expression by canceling out the \\( 47! \\) in the numerator and the denominator:\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{52 \\times 51 \\times 50 \\times 49 \\times 48}{5 \\times 4 \\times 3 \\times 2 \\times 1}\n\\]\n\nCalculating the numerator:\n\n\\[\n52 \\times 51 \\times 50 \\times 49 \\times 48 = 311,875,200\n\\]\n\nCalculating the denominator:\n\n\\[\n5 \\times 4 \\times 3 \\times 2 \\times 1 = 120\n\\]\n\nNow, divide the numerator by the denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{311,875,200}{120} = 2,598,960\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the number of possible distinct 5-card hands that can be dealt from a standard 52-card deck is \\(\\boxed{2,598,960}\\).'

There are \\(2!\\) (2 factorial) ways to arrange 2 people in 2 seats, which is 2.\n\nNow, we multiply the number of choices for each seat to find the total number of seating arrangements:\n\n\\[\n2 \\text{ (choices for driver)} \\times 3 \\text{ (choices for front passenger)} \\times 2 \\text{ (arrangements for back seats)} = 12\n\\]\n\nTherefore, there are \\(\\boxed{12}\\) possible seating arrangements."

From Condition I, \\(x = 1\\) satisfies the condition regardless of \\(y\\).\n   - For Condition II, \\(y\\) must be 1 (since \\(x = 1\\) does not satisfy Condition II by itself).\n   - So, the pair \\((1, 1)\\) satisfies both conditions.\n\n2. **Case 2: \\(y = 0\\)**\n   - From Condition I, \\(y = 0\\) satisfies the condition regardless of \\(x\\).\n   - For Condition II, \\(x\\) can be 0 or 2, or \\(y = 1\\) (but \\(y = 1\\) is not applicable here since \\(y = 0\\)).\n   - So, the pairs \\((0, 0)\\) and \\((2, 0)\\) satisfy both conditions.\n\n3. **Case 3: \\(y = 2\\)**\n   - From Condition I, \\(y = 2\\) satisfies the condition regardless of \\(x\\).\n   - For Condition II, \\(x\\) can be 0 or 2, or \\(y = 1\\) (but \\(y = 1\\) is not applicable here since \\(y = 2\\)).\n   - So, the pairs \\((0, 2)\\) and \\((2, 2)\\) satisfy both conditions.\n\n4. **Case 4: \\(x = 0\\)**\n   - From Condition II, \\(x = 0\\) satisfies the condition regardless of \\(y\\).\n   - For Condition I, \\(y\\) can be 0 or 2, or \\(x = 1\\) (but \\(x = 1\\) is not applicable here since \\(x = 0\\)).\n   - We have already counted \\((2, 0)\\) and \\((2, 2)\\) in previous cases.\n\n6. **Case 6: \\(y = 1\\)**\n   - From Condition II, \\(y = 1\\) satisfies the condition regardless of \\(x\\).\n   - For Condition I, \\(x\\) can be 1, or \\(y = 0\\) or 2 (but \\(y = 0\\) and \\(y = 2\\) are not applicable here since \\(y = 1\\)).\n   - 'To find the probability that a 5 is rolled on at least one of the two dice, we can use the complementary probability approach. First, we calculate the probability that a 5 is not rolled on either die, and then subtract this probability from 1.\n\n1. The probability that a 5 is not rolled on a single die is \\(\\frac{5}{6}\\), since there are 5 other outcomes (1, 2, 3, 4, or 6) out of 6 possible outcomes.\n\n2. The probability that a 5 is not rolled on both dice is \\(\\left(\\frac{5}{6}\\right) \\times \\left(\\frac{5}{6}\\right) = \\frac{25}{36}\\).\n\n3. Therefore, the probability that a 5 is rolled on at least one of the two dice is:\n   \\[\n   1 - \\frac{25}{36} = \\frac{36}{36} - \\frac{25}{36} = \\frac{11}{36}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the probability that a 5 is rolled on at least one of the two dice is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{11}{36}}\\).'
'To compute \\(\\binom{30}{27}\\), we can use the identity \\(\\binom{n}{k} = \\binom{n}{n-k}\\). This identity tells us that choosing \\(k\\) items from \\(n\\) is the same as choosing \\(n-k\\) items from \\(n\\).\n\nApplying this identity, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{30}{27} = \\binom{30}{30-27} = \\binom{30}{3}\n\\]\n\nNow, we compute \\(\\binom{30}{3}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\binom{30}{3} = \\frac{30 \\times 29 \\times 28}{3 \\times 2 \\times 1}\n\\]\n\nCalculating the numerator:\n\n\\[\n30 \\times 29 \\times 28 = 24360\n\\]\n\nCalculating the denominator:\n\n\\[\n3 \\times 2 \\times 1 = 6\n\\]\n\nNow, divide the numerator by the denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{24360}{6} = 4060\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\binom{30}{27} = \\boxed{4060}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the probability that exactly 2 out of the 4 people order a meat brat and the other 2 order hot dogs.\n\nFirst, let's calculate the total number of possible outcomes. Each person has 3 choices (meat brat, hot dog, or veggie brat), and there are 4 people, so the total number of possible outcomes is:\n\n\\[ 3^4 = 81. \\]\n\nNext, we need to find the number of favorable outcomes where exactly 2 people order a meat brat and the other 2 order hot dogs. \n\n1. **Choose 2 people to order a meat brat:** There are 4 people, and we need to choose 2 of them to order a meat brat. The number of ways to do this is given by the combination:\n\n   \\[ \\binom{4}{2} = 6. \\]\n\n2. **Assign the orders:** For the 2 people chosen to order a meat brat, there is only 1 way to assign them meat brats. For the remaining 2 people, there is only 1 way to assign them hot dogs.\n\nTherefore, the number of favorable outcomes is 6.\n\nFinally, the probability that exactly 2 people order a meat brat and the other 2 order hot dogs is the number of favorable outcomes divided by the total number of possible outcomes:\n\n\\[ \\frac{6}{81} = \\frac{2}{27}. \\]\n\nThus, the probability that 2 of them order a meat brat and the other two order hot dogs is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{2}{27}}\\)."

'To solve this problem, we need to count the natural numbers from 1 to 800 that contain the digit 6 at least twice. We will consider numbers with two 6s and numbers with three 6s separately.\n\n### Case 1: Numbers with exactly two 6s\n\n#### Three-digit numbers (100 to 799)\n- **Form: 66X**: The third digit \\(X\\) can be any digit from 0 to 9 except 6. So, there are 9 possibilities for \\(X\\).\n- **Form: 6X6**: The middle digit \\(X\\) can be any digit from 0 to 9 except 6. So, there are 9 possibilities for \\(X\\).\n- **Form: X66**: The first digit \\(X\\) can be any digit from 1 to 7 (since the number must be less than 800) except 6. So, there are 6 possibilities for \\(X\\).\n\nAdding these, we have \\(9 + 9 + 6 = 24\\) numbers with exactly two 6s in the range 100 to 799.\n\n#### Two-digit numbers (10 to 99)\n- **Form: 66**: There is only 1 number, which is 66.\n\nSo, there is 1 number with exactly two 6s in the range 10 to 99.\n\n### Case 2: Numbers with exactly three 6s\n\nThe only number in the range 1 to 800 with three 6s is 666. So, there is 1 such number.\n\n### Total Count\n\nAdding up all the cases, we have:\n- 24 numbers with exactly two 6s in the range 100 to 799.\n- 1 number with exactly two 6s in the range 10 to 99.\n- 1 number with exactly three 6s.\n\nThus, the total number of natural numbers from 1 to 800 that contain the digit 6 at least twice is \\(24 + 1 + 1 = 26\\).\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{26}\\).'
'The word "MISSISSIPPI" consists of 11 letters where some letters are repeated. Specifically, the letter \'M\' appears 1 time, \'I\' appears 4 times, \'S\' appears 4 times, and \'P\' appears 2 times. To find the number of distinct arrangements of the letters, we use the formula for permutations of multiset:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{n!}{n_1! \\times n_2! \\times \\cdots \\times n_k!}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( n \\) is the total number of letters, and \\( n_1, n_2, \\ldots, n_k \\) are the frequencies of the distinct letters.\n\nFor "MISSISSIPPI", we have:\n\n- Total letters, \\( n = 11 \\)\n- Frequency of \'M\', \\( n_1 = 1 \\)\n- Frequency of \'I\', \\( n_2 = 4 \\)\n- Frequency of \'S\', \\( n_3 = 4 \\)\n- Frequency of \'P\', \\( n_4 = 2 \\)\n\nPlugging these values into the formula, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{11!}{1! \\times 4! \\times 4! \\times 2!}\n\\]\n\nCalculating each factorial:\n\n- \\( 11! = 39916800 \\)\n- \\( 1! = 1 \\)\n- \\( 4! = 24 \\)\n- \\( 2! = 2 \\)\n\nNow, substitute these into the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{39916800}{1 \\times 24 \\times 24 \\times 2} = \\frac{39916800}{1152}\n\\]\n\nFinally, perform the division:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{39916800}{1152} = 34650\n\\]\n\nThus, the number of distinct ways to arrange the letters of the word "MISSISSIPPI" is \\(\\boxed{34650}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to find the ordered pairs \\((a, b)\\) such that \\(\\sqrt{a + \\sqrt{b}}\\) is an integer. Let's denote this integer by \\(k\\). Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{a + \\sqrt{b}} = k\n\\]\n\nSquaring both sides, we get:\n\n\\[\na + \\sqrt{b} = k^2\n\\]\n\nThis implies:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{b} = k^2 - a\n\\]\n\nFor \\(\\sqrt{b}\\) to be an integer, \\(b\\) must be a perfect square. Let \\(\\sqrt{b} = m\\), where \\(m\\) is an integer. Thus, we have:\n\n\\[\nb = m^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting back, we get:\n\n\\[\nm = k^2 - a\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to ensure that \\(a\\) and \\(b\\) satisfy the given constraints: \\(1 \\le a \\le 10\\) and \\(1 \\le b \\le 36\\). Since \\(b = m^2\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n1 \\le m^2 \\le 36\n\\]\n\nThis implies:\n\n\\[\n1 \\le m \\le 6\n\\]\n\nFor each integer \\(m\\) from 1 to 6, we have:\n\n\\[\nk^2 - a = m\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\n\\[\na = k^2 - m\n\\]\n\nWe need \\(a\\) to satisfy \\(1 \\le a \\le 10\\). Therefore:\n\n\\[\n1 \\le k^2 - m \\le 10\n\\]\n\nRearranging gives:\n\n\\[\nm \\le k^2 - 1\n\\]\n\nand\n\n\\[\nk^2 - 10 \\le m\n\\]\n\nCombining these inequalities, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\max(1, k^2 - 10) \\le m \\le \\min(6, k^2 - 1)\n\\]\n\nWe need to find integer values of \\(k\\) such that there exists at least one integer \\(m\\) satisfying the above inequality. Let's consider possible values of \\(k\\):\n\n1. **For \\(k = 2\\):**\n   - \\(k^2 = 4\\)\n   - \\(\\max(1, 4 - 10) = 1\\)\n   - \\(\\min(6, 4 - 1) = 3\\)\n   - Possible values of \\(m\\): 1, 2, 3\n   - Corresponding \\(a\\): \\(3, 2, 1\\)\n\n2. **For \\(k = 3\\):**\n   - \\(k^2 = 9\\)\n   - \\(\\max(1, 9 - 10) = 1\\)\n   - \\(\\min(6, 9 - 1) = 6\\)\n   - Possible values of \\(m\\): 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6\n   - Corresponding \\(a\\): \\(8, 7, 6, 5, 4, 3\\)\n\n3. **For \\(k = 4\\):**\n   - \\(k^2 = 16\\)\n   - \\(\\max(1, 16 - 10) = 6\\)\n   - \\(\\min(6, 16 - 1) = 6\\)\n   - Possible value of \\(m\\): 6\n   - Corresponding \\(a\\): \\(10\\)\n\nNow, let's count the number of valid \\((a, b)\\) pairs:\n\n- For \\(k = 2\\), we have 3 pairs: \\((3, 1), (2, 4), (1, 9)\\)\n- For \\(k = 3\\), we have 6 pairs: \\((8, 1), (7, 4), (6, 9), (5, 16), (4, 25), (3, 36)\\)\n- For \\(k = 4\\), we have 1 pair: \\((10, 36)\\)\n\nAdding these, the total number of ordered pairs \\((a, b)\\) is:\n\n\\[\n3 + 6 + 1 = 10\n\\]\n\nThus, the number of ordered pairs \\((a, b)\\) such that \\(\\sqrt{a + \\sqrt{b}}\\) is an integer is \\(\\boxed{10}\\)."
