'To solve this problem, we need to select a chairman and then choose any subset of the remaining candidates to be members-at-large.\n\n1. **Choose the Chairman**: There are 10 candidates, so there are 10 possible choices for the chairman.\n\n2. **Choose the Members-at-Large**: After selecting the chairman, there are 9 candidates left. For the members-at-large, we can choose any subset of these 9 candidates, including the possibility of choosing no members at all. The number of subsets of a set with 9 elements is \\(2^9\\).\n\nTherefore, the total number of ways to form the committee is the product of the number of ways to choose the chairman and the number of ways to choose the members-at-large:\n\n\\[\n10 \\times 2^9 = 10 \\times 512 = 5120\n\\]\n\nThus, there are \\(\\boxed{5120}\\) ways to form the committee.'
'To find how many prime numbers have squares between 100 and 300, we need to determine the range of integers whose squares fall within this interval.\n\nFirst, we find the smallest integer \\( n \\) such that \\( n^2 > 100 \\):\n\\[\nn^2 > 100 \\implies n > \\sqrt{100} = 10\n\\]\nThus, \\( n \\) must be greater than 10. The smallest integer greater than 10 is 11.\n\nNext, we find the largest integer \\( m \\) such that \\( m^2 < 300 \\):\n\\[\nm^2 < 300 \\implies m < \\sqrt{300} \\approx 17.32\n\\]\nThus, \\( m \\) must be less than 17.32. The largest integer less than 17.32 is 17.\n\nNow, we consider the integers from 11 to 17 and determine which of these are prime:\n- 11 is prime.\n- 12 is not prime (divisible by 2 and 3).\n- 13 is prime.\n- 14 is not prime (divisible by 2 and 7).\n- 15 is not prime (divisible by 3 and 5).\n- 16 is not prime (divisible by 2).\n- 17 is prime.\n\nThe prime numbers between 11 and 17 are 11, 13, and 17. Therefore, there are 3 prime numbers whose squares are between 100 and 300.\n\nThus, the answer is \\(\\boxed{3}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the probability that 3 randomly chosen cards from a standard 52-card deck can be arranged into a sequence of three consecutive cards of the same suit.\n\nFirst, let's consider the number of possible sequences of three consecutive cards in a single suit. In a standard deck, each suit has 13 cards: Ace, 2, 3, ..., 10, Jack, Queen, King. The possible sequences of three consecutive cards are:\n\n1. Ace, 2, 3\n2. 2, 3, 4\n3. 3, 4, 5\n4. 4, 5, 6\n5. 5, 6, 7\n6. 6, 7, 8\n7. 7, 8, 9\n8. 8, 9, 10\n9. 9, 10, Jack\n10. 10, Jack, Queen\n11. Jack, Queen, King\n12. Queen, King, Ace\n\nThere are 12 possible sequences for each suit. Since there are 4 suits, the total number of sequences is \\(12 \\times 4 = 48\\).\n\nNext, we calculate the total number of ways to choose 3 cards from a 52-card deck. This is given by the combination \\(\\binom{52}{3}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\binom{52}{3} = \\frac{52 \\times 51 \\times 50}{3 \\times 2 \\times 1} = 22,100\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to determine the number of favorable outcomes, which is the number of ways to choose 3 cards that can form one of the 48 sequences. For each sequence, there is exactly 1 way to choose the 3 cards (since the order within the sequence doesn't matter), so there are 48 favorable outcomes.\n\nFinally, the probability that the 3 chosen cards can be arranged into a group of three consecutive cards of the same suit is the ratio of favorable outcomes to the total number of outcomes:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Probability} = \\frac{48}{22,100} = \\frac{24}{11,050}\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{24}{11,050}}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we first need to determine the total number of possible outcomes and then count the number of favorable outcomes where the product of the numbers picked by Krishanu and Shaunak is more than 10.\n\n1. **Total number of outcomes:**\n   Each person can pick an integer between 1 and 10, inclusive. Therefore, there are 10 choices for Krishanu and 10 choices for Shaunak. The total number of possible outcomes is:\n   \\[\n   10 \\times 10 = 100\n   \\]\n\n2. **Favorable outcomes:**\n   We need to count the number of pairs \\((a, b)\\) such that \\(1 \\leq a, b \\leq 10\\) and \\(a \\times b > 10\\).\n\n   - If \\(a = 1\\), then \\(b\\) must be greater than 10, which is impossible since \\(b \\leq 10\\). So, there are 0 favorable outcomes for \\(a = 1\\).\n   - If \\(a = 2\\), then \\(b\\) must be greater than 5. So, \\(b\\) can be 6, 7, 8, 9, or 10. This gives 5 favorable outcomes.\n   - If \\(a = 3\\), then \\(b\\) must be greater than \\(\\frac{10}{3} \\approx 3.33\\). So, \\(b\\) can be 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, or 10. This gives 7 favorable outcomes.\n   - If \\(a = 4\\), then \\(b\\) must be greater than 2.5. So, \\(b\\) can be 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, or 10. This gives 8 favorable outcomes.\n   - If \\(a = 5\\), then \\(b\\) must be greater than 2. So, \\(b\\) can be 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, or 10. This gives 8 favorable outcomes.\n   - If \\(a = 6\\), then \\(b\\) must be greater than \\(\\frac{10}{6} \\approx 1.67\\). So, \\(b\\) can be 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, or 10. This gives 9 favorable outcomes.\n   - If \\(a = 7\\), then \\(b\\) must be greater than \\(\\frac{10}{7} \\approx 1.43\\). So, \\(b\\) can be 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, or 10. This gives 9 favorable outcomes.\n   - If \\(a = 8\\), then \\(b\\) must be greater than 1.25. So, \\(b\\) can be 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, or 10. This gives 9 favorable outcomes.\n   - If \\(a = 9\\), then \\(b\\) must be greater than \\(\\frac{10}{9} \\approx 1.11\\). So, \\(b\\) can be 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, or 10. This gives 9 favorable outcomes.\n   - If \\(a = 10\\), then \\(b\\) must be greater than 1. So, \\(b\\) can be 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, or 10. This gives 9 favorable outcomes.\n\n   Adding these up, the total number of favorable outcomes is:\n   \\[\n   0 + 5 + 7 + 8 + 8 + 9 + 9 + 9 + 9 + 9 = 73\n   \\]\n\n3. **Probability:**\n   The probability that the product of their numbers is more than 10 is the number of favorable outcomes divided by the total number of outcomes:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{73}{100}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the probability that the product of their numbers is more than 10 is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{73}{100}}\\).'
'To determine the number of ways the three planes can take off, we need to consider both the order of the takeoffs and the choice of runway for each takeoff.\n\n1. **Order of Takeoffs**: There are 3 planes, so there are \\(3! = 6\\) possible orders in which the planes can take off.\n\n2. **Choice of Runway**: For each takeoff, there are 2 runways available. Therefore, for each of the 3 takeoffs, there are 2 choices of runway.\n\nThus, the total number of ways to schedule the takeoffs is given by multiplying the number of orders by the number of runway choices for each takeoff:\n\n\\[\n6 \\text{ (orders)} \\times 2^3 \\text{ (runway choices)} = 6 \\times 8 = 48\n\\]\n\nTherefore, there are \\(\\boxed{48}\\) ways to schedule the takeoffs of the three planes.'
'A five-digit palindrome has the form \\(abcba\\), where \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\) are digits. Since the number must read the same forward and backward, the first and last digits must be the same (\\(a\\)), and the second and fourth digits must be the same (\\(b\\)).\n\nGiven that the digits can be 1, 2, or 9, we can choose each of \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\) independently from these three digits.\n\n- For \\(a\\), there are 3 choices: 1, 2, or 9.\n- For \\(b\\), there are 3 choices: 1, 2, or 9.\n- For \\(c\\), there are 3 choices: 1, 2, or 9.\n\nTherefore, the total number of five-digit palindromes that can be formed is the product of the number of choices for each digit:\n\n\\[\n3 \\times 3 \\times 3 = 27\n\\]\n\nThus, there are \\(\\boxed{27}\\) five-digit palindromes that can be made from the digits 1, 2, and 9.'
"In the \\( n \\)th row of Pascal's Triangle, the elements are the binomial coefficients \\(\\binom{n}{0}, \\binom{n}{1}, \\ldots, \\binom{n}{n}\\). The sum of these elements is \\(2^n\\).\n\nThe arithmetic mean of the elements in the \\( n \\)th row is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2^n}{n+1}\n\\]\n\nWe are told that this mean is 51.2, so we have the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2^n}{n+1} = 51.2\n\\]\n\nMultiplying both sides by \\( n+1 \\) gives:\n\n\\[\n2^n = 51.2 \\times (n+1)\n\\]\n\nSince 51.2 can be expressed as a fraction, \\( 51.2 = \\frac{512}{10} = \\frac{256}{5} \\), we can rewrite the equation as:\n\n\\[\n2^n = \\frac{256}{5} \\times (n+1)\n\\]\n\nTo eliminate the fraction, multiply both sides by 5:\n\n\\[\n5 \\times 2^n = 256 \\times (n+1)\n\\]\n\nNow, let's test some values of \\( n \\) to find a solution:\n\n- For \\( n = 8 \\):\n\n  \\[\n  2^8 = 256\n  \\]\n\n  \\[\n  5 \\times 256 = 1280\n  \\]\n\n  \\[\n  256 \\times (8+1) = 256 \\times 9 = 2304\n  \\]\n\n  This does not satisfy the equation.\n\n- For \\( n = 9 \\):\n\n  \\[\n  2^9 = 512\n  \\]\n\n  \\[\n  5 \\times 512 = 2560\n  \\]\n\n  \\[\n  256 \\times (9+1) = 256 \\times 10 = 2560\n  \\]\n\n  This satisfies the equation.\n\nThus, the value of \\( n \\) is \\(\\boxed{9}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to determine the probability that Kendra lands on spaces such that the sum of the numbers is exactly 30 after three turns. Let\'s analyze the board and the possible moves:\n\n1. **Initial Position**: Kendra starts at the center of the board, which is the "Start" square with no numerical value.\n\n2. **Possible Moves**: Each turn, Kendra can move in one of four directions: Up, Down, Left, or Right. Each direction is equally likely, with a probability of \\(\\frac{1}{4}\\).\n\n3. **Board Layout**: The board is symmetric, and the numbers on the board are:\n   - 10 on the immediate adjacent squares (Up, Down, Left, Right).\n   - 5 on the diagonal squares one step away (e.g., Up-Right, Up-Left, etc.).\n   - 20 on the squares two steps away in the cardinal directions (e.g., Up-Up, Down-Down, etc.).\n   - 15 on the squares two steps away in a combination of cardinal and diagonal directions (e.g., Up-Up-Right, etc.).\n\n4. **Objective**: We need to find the probability that the sum of the numbers on which Kendra lands after three turns is exactly 30.\n\nLet\'s consider the possible sequences of moves that result in a sum of 30:\n\n- **Sequence 1**: 10, 10, 10\n  - Probability: \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{4}\\right)^3 = \\frac{1}{64}\\)\n\n- **Sequence 2**: 10, 5, 15\n  - Probability: \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{4}\\right)^3 = \\frac{1}{64}\\)\n\n- **Sequence 3**: 10, 15, 5\n  - Probability: \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{4}\\right)^3 = \\frac{1}{64}\\)\n\n- **Sequence 4**: 5, 10, 15\n  - Probability: \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{4}\\right)^3 = \\frac{1}{64}\\)\n\n- **Sequence 5**: 5, 15, 10\n  - Probability: \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{4}\\right)^3 = \\frac{1}{64}\\)\n\n- **Sequence 6**: 15, 10, 5\n  - Probability: \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{4}\\right)^3 = \\frac{1}{64}\\)\n\n- **Sequence 7**: 15, 5, 10\n  - Probability: \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{4}\\right)^3 = \\frac{1}{64}\\)\n\nEach sequence has a probability of \\(\\frac{1}{64}\\), and there are 7 such sequences. Therefore, the total probability is:\n\n\\[\n7 \\times \\frac{1}{64} = \\frac{7}{64}\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability that the sum of the numbers in the spaces on which Kendra will land is exactly 30 after her third complete turn is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{7}{64}}\\).'
'The probability that it will not rain tomorrow is the complement of the probability that it will rain. If the probability of rain is \\(\\frac{1}{11}\\), then the probability that it will not rain is:\n\n\\[ 1 - \\frac{1}{11} = \\frac{11}{11} - \\frac{1}{11} = \\frac{10}{11}. \\]\n\nThus, the probability that it will not rain tomorrow is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{10}{11}}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to calculate the probability of selecting exactly two freshmen girls and three freshmen boys from the class.\n\nFirst, let's identify the numbers involved:\n- There are 15 girls, of which 12 are freshmen.\n- There are 15 boys, of which 11 are freshmen.\n\nWe want to select 2 freshmen girls and 3 freshmen boys from the class.\n\n1. **Calculate the number of ways to choose 2 freshmen girls from 12 freshmen girls:**\n   \\[\n   \\binom{12}{2} = \\frac{12 \\times 11}{2 \\times 1} = 66\n   \\]\n\n2. **Calculate the number of ways to choose 3 freshmen boys from 11 freshmen boys:**\n   \\[\n   \\binom{11}{3} = \\frac{11 \\times 10 \\times 9}{3 \\times 2 \\times 1} = 165\n   \\]\n\n3. **Calculate the total number of ways to choose 5 students from the entire class of 30 students:**\n   \\[\n   \\binom{30}{5} = \\frac{30 \\times 29 \\times 28 \\times 27 \\times 26}{5 \\times 4 \\times 3 \\times 2 \\times 1} = 142506\n   \\]\n\n4. **Calculate the probability of selecting 2 freshmen girls and 3 freshmen boys:**\n   \\[\n   \\text{Probability} = \\frac{\\binom{12}{2} \\times \\binom{11}{3}}{\\binom{30}{5}} = \\frac{66 \\times 165}{142506}\n   \\]\n\n5. **Calculate the result:**\n   \\[\n   \\frac{66 \\times 165}{142506} = \\frac{10890}{142506} \\approx 0.076\n   \\]\n\nThus, the probability that in a randomly selected group of five students there will be two freshmen girls and three freshmen boys is approximately \\( \\boxed{0.076} \\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the number of different dinners that can be created with the given constraints and ensure that this number is at least 365, the number of days in the year 2003.\n\nLet's denote the number of main courses as \\( m \\). According to the problem, the number of appetizers is twice the number of main courses, so the number of appetizers is \\( 2m \\). The number of desserts is given as 3.\n\nA dinner consists of one appetizer, one main course, and one dessert. Therefore, the total number of different dinners that can be created is the product of the number of choices for each course:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Total number of dinners} = (\\text{number of appetizers}) \\times (\\text{number of main courses}) \\times (\\text{number of desserts}) = 2m \\times m \\times 3 = 6m^2\n\\]\n\nWe want this number to be at least 365:\n\n\\[\n6m^2 \\geq 365\n\\]\n\nTo find the smallest integer \\( m \\) that satisfies this inequality, we solve for \\( m \\):\n\n\\[\nm^2 \\geq \\frac{365}{6} \\approx 60.8333\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides, we get:\n\n\\[\nm \\geq \\sqrt{60.8333} \\approx 7.8\n\\]\n\nSince \\( m \\) must be an integer, we round up to the nearest whole number, which is 8.\n\nTherefore, the least number of main courses that the restaurant should offer is \\(\\boxed{8}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to consider the arrangement of 6 people around a circular table with 7 seats, where rotations of the same arrangement are considered identical.\n\nFirst, we choose 6 seats out of the 7 available seats to place the people. The number of ways to choose 6 seats from 7 is given by the combination \\(\\binom{7}{6} = 7\\).\n\nOnce the seats are chosen, we need to arrange the 6 people in those seats. In a circular arrangement, we fix one person to break the rotational symmetry, and then arrange the remaining 5 people. The number of ways to arrange 5 people is \\(5!\\).\n\nTherefore, the total number of distinct arrangements is given by:\n\\[\n7 \\times 5! = 7 \\times 120 = 840\n\\]\n\nThus, there are \\(\\boxed{840}\\) ways to arrange 6 people around a circular table with 7 seats.'
"To solve this problem, we first need to determine the total number of ways to select two different numbers from the set \\(\\{1, 2, 3, 4\\}\\). There are 4 numbers in the set, and we are choosing 2, so the total number of combinations is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{4}{2} = 6\n\\]\n\nThe possible pairs of numbers are: \\((1, 2)\\), \\((1, 3)\\), \\((1, 4)\\), \\((2, 3)\\), \\((2, 4)\\), and \\((3, 4)\\).\n\nNext, we determine which of these pairs result in an even product. A product is even if at least one of the numbers in the pair is even. The even numbers in the set are 2 and 4.\n\nLet's check each pair:\n\n- \\((1, 2)\\): Product is \\(1 \\times 2 = 2\\), which is even.\n- \\((1, 3)\\): Product is \\(1 \\times 3 = 3\\), which is odd.\n- \\((1, 4)\\): Product is \\(1 \\times 4 = 4\\), which is even.\n- \\((2, 3)\\): Product is \\(2 \\times 3 = 6\\), which is even.\n- \\((2, 4)\\): Product is \\(2 \\times 4 = 8\\), which is even.\n- \\((3, 4)\\): Product is \\(3 \\times 4 = 12\\), which is even.\n\nOut of the 6 possible pairs, 5 pairs result in an even product: \\((1, 2)\\), \\((1, 4)\\), \\((2, 3)\\), \\((2, 4)\\), and \\((3, 4)\\).\n\nTherefore, the probability that the product is even is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{5}{6}\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability that the product is even is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{5}{6}}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the largest and smallest possible values for the number in the top cell, given that we are using three different one-digit positive integers in the bottom row.\n\nLet's denote the three integers as \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\), where \\(1 \\leq a < b < c \\leq 9\\).\n\nThe process of filling the cells is as follows:\n1. The bottom row consists of \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\).\n2. The second row consists of \\(a+b\\) and \\(b+c\\).\n3. The top cell contains the sum of the two numbers in the second row: \\((a+b) + (b+c) = a + 2b + c\\).\n\nWe need to find the maximum and minimum values of \\(a + 2b + c\\).\n\n**Maximum Value:**\nTo maximize \\(a + 2b + c\\), we should choose the largest possible values for \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\). The largest one-digit integers are 7, 8, and 9. Thus, we choose \\(a = 7\\), \\(b = 8\\), and \\(c = 9\\).\n\nCalculating the top cell value:\n\\[\na + 2b + c = 7 + 2(8) + 9 = 7 + 16 + 9 = 32\n\\]\n\n**Minimum Value:**\nTo minimize \\(a + 2b + c\\), we should choose the smallest possible values for \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\). The smallest one-digit integers are 1, 2, and 3. Thus, we choose \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = 2\\), and \\(c = 3\\).\n\nCalculating the top cell value:\n\\[\na + 2b + c = 1 + 2(2) + 3 = 1 + 4 + 3 = 8\n\\]\n\n**Difference:**\nThe difference between the largest and smallest possible values in the top cell is:\n\\[\n32 - 8 = 24\n\\]\n\nThus, the difference between the largest and smallest numbers possible in the top cell is \\(\\boxed{24}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to calculate the probability that a randomly chosen subcommittee of 3 members consists of 1 Republican, 1 Democrat, and 1 Independent.\n\nFirst, we calculate the total number of ways to choose a subcommittee of 3 members from the 13 members (5 Republicans, 6 Democrats, and 2 Independents) in the Senate committee. This is given by the combination:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{13}{3} = \\frac{13 \\times 12 \\times 11}{3 \\times 2 \\times 1} = 286\n\\]\n\nNext, we calculate the number of ways to choose 1 Republican, 1 Democrat, and 1 Independent:\n\n- The number of ways to choose 1 Republican from 5 is \\(\\binom{5}{1} = 5\\).\n- The number of ways to choose 1 Democrat from 6 is \\(\\binom{6}{1} = 6\\).\n- The number of ways to choose 1 Independent from 2 is \\(\\binom{2}{1} = 2\\).\n\nThe total number of ways to choose 1 Republican, 1 Democrat, and 1 Independent is:\n\n\\[\n5 \\times 6 \\times 2 = 60\n\\]\n\nFinally, the probability that the subcommittee consists of 1 Republican, 1 Democrat, and 1 Independent is the ratio of the number of favorable outcomes to the total number of outcomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{60}{286} = \\frac{30}{143}\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{30}{143}}\\).'
'To find the expected value of the sum of the rolls of two fair eight-sided dice, we first need to determine the expected value of a single die roll. \n\nFor a single eight-sided die, the possible outcomes are the numbers 1 through 8. The expected value \\( E \\) of a single die roll is calculated as the average of these outcomes:\n\n\\[\nE = \\frac{1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5 + 6 + 7 + 8}{8} = \\frac{36}{8} = 4.5\n\\]\n\nSince the two dice are independent, the expected value of the sum of the rolls of both dice is the sum of their individual expected values. Therefore, the expected value of the sum is:\n\n\\[\nE(\\text{sum}) = E(\\text{die 1}) + E(\\text{die 2}) = 4.5 + 4.5 = 9\n\\]\n\nThus, the expected value of the sum of the rolls of both dice is \\(\\boxed{9}\\).'
"To find the number of students who did no math, no English, and no science homework, we can use the principle of inclusion-exclusion. Let's define the following sets:\n\n- \\( M \\): the set of students who did math homework\n- \\( E \\): the set of students who did English homework\n- \\( S \\): the set of students who did science homework\n\nWe are given the following information:\n\n- \\(|M| = 59\\)\n- \\(|E| = 49\\)\n- \\(|S| = 42\\)\n- \\(|E \\cap S| = 20\\)\n- \\(|S \\cap M| = 29\\)\n- \\(|M \\cap E| = 31\\)\n- \\(|M \\cap E \\cap S| = 12\\)\n\nWe want to find the number of students who did no homework in any of the subjects, which is the complement of the union of the three sets, \\(|M \\cup E \\cup S|^c\\).\n\nUsing the principle of inclusion-exclusion, we have:\n\n\\[\n|M \\cup E \\cup S| = |M| + |E| + |S| - |M \\cap E| - |E \\cap S| - |S \\cap M| + |M \\cap E \\cap S|\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given values:\n\n\\[\n|M \\cup E \\cup S| = 59 + 49 + 42 - 31 - 20 - 29 + 12\n\\]\n\nCalculating step-by-step:\n\n\\[\n59 + 49 + 42 = 150\n\\]\n\\[\n31 + 20 + 29 = 80\n\\]\n\\[\n150 - 80 + 12 = 82\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(|M \\cup E \\cup S| = 82\\).\n\nThe number of students who did no homework in any of the subjects is:\n\n\\[\n100 - |M \\cup E \\cup S| = 100 - 82 = 18\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the number of students who did no math, no English, and no science homework is \\(\\boxed{18}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we can use the complementary counting principle. First, we calculate the total number of 5-digit numbers, and then subtract the number of 5-digit numbers that do not contain the digits 2 or 3.\n\n1. **Total number of 5-digit numbers:**\n\n   A 5-digit number ranges from 10000 to 99999. The first digit (ten-thousands place) can be any digit from 1 to 9 (9 options), and each of the remaining four digits (thousands, hundreds, tens, and units places) can be any digit from 0 to 9 (10 options each). Therefore, the total number of 5-digit numbers is:\n\n   \\[\n   9 \\times 10 \\times 10 \\times 10 \\times 10 = 9 \\times 10^4 = 90000\n   \\]\n\n2. **Number of 5-digit numbers without a 2 or 3:**\n\n   If a number does not contain the digits 2 or 3, then each digit can be one of the remaining 8 digits: 0, 1, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, or 9. The first digit (ten-thousands place) can be any digit from 1, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, or 9 (7 options), and each of the remaining four digits can be any of the 8 digits. Therefore, the number of 5-digit numbers without a 2 or 3 is:\n\n   \\[\n   7 \\times 8 \\times 8 \\times 8 \\times 8 = 7 \\times 8^4\n   \\]\n\n   Calculating \\(8^4\\):\n\n   \\[\n   8^4 = 8 \\times 8 \\times 8 \\times 8 = 64 \\times 64 = 4096\n   \\]\n\n   Therefore, the number of 5-digit numbers without a 2 or 3 is:\n\n   \\[\n   7 \\times 4096 = 28672\n   \\]\n\n3. **Number of 5-digit numbers with at least one 2 or 3:**\n\n   Subtract the number of 5-digit numbers without a 2 or 3 from the total number of 5-digit numbers:\n\n   \\[\n   90000 - 28672 = 61328\n   \\]\n\nThus, the number of 5-digit numbers that have at least one 2 or one 3 among their digits is \\(\\boxed{61328}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to consider the constraints imposed by the two lines of symmetry on the 3x3 grid. The grid has both vertical and diagonal lines of symmetry, which means that certain squares must be the same color to maintain symmetry.\n\nLet's label the squares of the grid as follows:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{array}{ccc}\nA & B & C \\\\\nD & E & F \\\\\nG & H & I \\\\\n\\end{array}\n\\]\n\n1. **Vertical Line of Symmetry**: This line runs through the middle column, so the left and right columns must be mirror images. This means:\n   - \\(A = C\\)\n   - \\(D = F\\)\n   - \\(G = I\\)\n\n2. **Diagonal Line of Symmetry**: This line runs from the top left to the bottom right, so the grid must be symmetric along this diagonal. This means:\n   - \\(A = I\\)\n   - \\(B = H\\)\n   - \\(C = G\\)\n\nCombining these conditions, we have:\n- From the vertical symmetry: \\(A = C\\), \\(D = F\\), \\(G = I\\)\n- From the diagonal symmetry: \\(A = I\\), \\(B = H\\), \\(C = G\\)\n\nFrom \\(A = I\\) and \\(G = I\\), we have \\(A = G = I\\). Since \\(A = C\\) and \\(C = G\\), we also have \\(A = C = G = I\\).\n\nFrom \\(B = H\\), we have \\(B = H\\).\n\nFrom \\(D = F\\), we have \\(D = F\\).\n\nNow, let's count the distinct colors:\n- \\(A = C = G = I\\) is one color.\n- \\(B = H\\) is a second color.\n- \\(D = F\\) is a third color.\n- \\(E\\) can be a fourth color, as it is not constrained by the symmetry.\n\nThus, the maximum number of different colors that can be used while maintaining the symmetry is 4. Therefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{4}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we first need to identify the prime numbers on a standard six-sided die. The numbers on a die are 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, and 6. Among these, the prime numbers are 2, 3, and 5.\n\nNext, we determine the numbers that are not prime: 1, 4, and 6.\n\nThe probability of not rolling a prime number on one die is the probability of rolling one of these non-prime numbers. There are 3 non-prime numbers (1, 4, and 6) out of 6 possible outcomes on a die, so the probability of not rolling a prime number on one die is \\(\\frac{3}{6} = \\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\nSince the dice rolls are independent, the probability of not rolling a prime number on both dice is the product of the probabilities for each die:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} \\times \\frac{1}{2} = \\frac{1}{4}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the probability that Adam did not roll a prime number on either die is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{4}}\\).'
"To find the probability that Ryan beats Matt in a landslide by choosing 3 Paper cards, we need to determine the probability that Ryan randomly selects 3 Paper cards from his deck of 5 Paper cards and 3 Scissors cards.\n\nRyan's deck has a total of 8 cards (5 Paper and 3 Scissors). The number of ways to choose 3 cards from these 8 cards is given by the combination \\(\\binom{8}{3}\\).\n\nThe number of ways to choose 3 Paper cards from the 5 Paper cards is given by the combination \\(\\binom{5}{3}\\).\n\nThe probability that Ryan selects 3 Paper cards is then the ratio of these two combinations:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Probability} = \\frac{\\binom{5}{3}}{\\binom{8}{3}}\n\\]\n\nCalculating these combinations:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{5}{3} = \\frac{5 \\times 4}{2 \\times 1} = 10\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\binom{8}{3} = \\frac{8 \\times 7 \\times 6}{3 \\times 2 \\times 1} = 56\n\\]\n\nThus, the probability is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{10}{56} = \\frac{5}{28}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the probability that Ryan beats Matt in a landslide by choosing 3 Paper cards is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{5}{28}}\\)."
'To find the coefficient of \\(x^2\\) in the expansion of \\(\\left(x+\\frac{1}{x}\\right)^6\\), we can use the binomial theorem. The binomial theorem states that:\n\n\\[\n(a+b)^n = \\sum_{k=0}^{n} \\binom{n}{k} a^{n-k} b^k\n\\]\n\nIn this case, \\(a = x\\), \\(b = \\frac{1}{x}\\), and \\(n = 6\\). We are looking for the term where the power of \\(x\\) is 2. In the general term of the expansion, \\(\\binom{6}{k} x^{6-k} \\left(\\frac{1}{x}\\right)^k\\), the power of \\(x\\) is:\n\n\\[\n6-k-k = 6-2k\n\\]\n\nWe want this power to be 2, so we set up the equation:\n\n\\[\n6-2k = 2\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(k\\), we get:\n\n\\[\n6 - 2k = 2 \\\\\n4 = 2k \\\\\nk = 2\n\\]\n\nNow, we substitute \\(k = 2\\) into the binomial coefficient and the term:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{6}{2} x^{6-2} \\left(\\frac{1}{x}\\right)^2 = \\binom{6}{2} x^4 \\cdot \\frac{1}{x^2} = \\binom{6}{2} x^2\n\\]\n\nCalculating the binomial coefficient:\n\n\\[\n\\binom{6}{2} = \\frac{6 \\times 5}{2 \\times 1} = 15\n\\]\n\nThus, the coefficient of \\(x^2\\) in the expansion is \\(\\boxed{15}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to divide 12 dimes into three piles such that each pile contains an odd number of dimes. Let's denote the number of dimes in the three piles as \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\), where \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\) are odd numbers and \\(a + b + c = 12\\).\n\nThe odd numbers that sum to 12 must be chosen from the set of odd numbers less than or equal to 12: \\(\\{1, 3, 5, 7, 9, 11\\}\\). However, since the sum of three odd numbers is odd, and 12 is even, we need to ensure that the sum of the three numbers is even. This means we need to find combinations of three odd numbers that sum to 12.\n\nThe possible odd numbers that can be used are 1, 3, 5, 7, 9, and 11. We need to find combinations of these numbers that add up to 12. Let's consider the possibilities:\n\n1. \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = 5\\), \\(c = 6\\) (not possible since 6 is not odd)\n2. \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = 3\\), \\(c = 8\\) (not possible since 8 is not odd)\n3. \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = 7\\), \\(c = 4\\) (not possible since 4 is not odd)\n4. \\(a = 3\\), \\(b = 5\\), \\(c = 4\\) (not possible since 4 is not odd)\n5. \\(a = 3\\), \\(b = 3\\), \\(c = 6\\) (not possible since 6 is not odd)\n6. \\(a = 5\\), \\(b = 5\\), \\(c = 2\\) (not possible since 2 is not odd)\n\nUpon checking all possible combinations, we find that there are no combinations of three odd numbers that sum to 12. Therefore, it is impossible to divide 12 dimes into three piles with each pile containing an odd number of dimes.\n\nThus, the number of different ways to divide 12 dimes into three piles with an odd number of dimes in each pile is \\(\\boxed{0}\\)."
